He needs to know the physical properties of the evidence (compressibility of soil, hydraulic conductivity) to run his simulations and explain *how* the crime happened.\n            *   Limitation: He often doesn\u0027t have enough data on those physical properties and has to make assumptions.\n\n4.  **Connect to the User\u0027s Provided Papers:**\n    *   Explicitly go through the papers and state *why* they don\u0027t do this.\n        *   The GWR/MGWR/SM-GWR/GTNNWR papers are all about finding relationships between subsidence (the effect) and **external driving forces** (the causes, like population, rainfall, buildings). A **\"scorecard\"** to know how well you\u0027re doing: The **Loss Function** (or cost function).\n        2.  A way to know **\"which direction to go\"** to improve your score: The **Gradient** (calculated via **Backpropagation**).\n        3.  A way to decide **\"how big a step to take\"** in that direction: The **Learning Rate**.\n    *   The whole process is **iterative**. You don\u0027t get the answer in one shot. It\u0027s a continuous process of guessing, checking the score, finding a better direction, and taking a small step.\n\n3.  **Structure the Answer with an Analogy:**\n    *   The user responded well to analogies before. What\u0027s a good analogy for gradient descent?\n        *   **A hiker lost on a foggy mountain at night.** This is a classic and very effective one.\n            *   **Goal:** Find the lowest point in the valley (minimize the loss).\n            *   **Constraint:** It\u0027s foggy and dark; you can\u0027t see the whole landscape. You can only feel the ground right under your feet.\n            *   **Action:**\n                1.  Feel the slope of the ground (calculate the gradient).\n                2.  Take a small step in the steepest downward direction (update the weights using the gradient and learning rate).\n                3.  Repeat.\n    *   This analogy perfectly captures the core concepts: minimizing a function (loss), being local (only knowing the gradient at your current position), iterative steps, and the importance of step size (learning rate).\n\n4.  **Flesh out the \"Hiker\" Analogy into a Step-by-Step Explanation:**\n    *   **Step 0: The Setup (Kh·ªüi ƒë·ªông v√† ƒêo√°n M√≤ L·∫ßn ƒê·∫ßu):**\n        *   Explain what \"weights\" are in the neural network. C√°i n√†y ƒë·ªÉ anh bi·∫øt ch√≠nh x√°c ƒëo·∫°n n√†o l√† \"nguy√™n t√°c\" c·ªßa em, anh s·∫Ω t·∫≠p trung v√†o ƒë√≥ m√† kh√¥ng nh·∫ßm l·∫´n v·ªõi l·ªùi c·ªßa em.\n\n---\n\n### **V√≠ d·ª• Th·ª±c t·∫ø n√≥ s·∫Ω tr√¥ng nh∆∞ v·∫ßy:**\n\nOk anh, ƒë√¢y l√† ph·∫ßn Abstract c·ªßa em, anh xem gi√∫p nh√©.\n\\---\n**Abstract:**\n\nLand subsidence, driven by excessive groundwater withdrawal, poses a significant threat to the Choushui River Fluvial Plain in Taiwan. While Geographically Weighted Regression (GWR) models have been used, they often fail to capture the complex non-linear and spatiotemporal nature of subsurface processes. This study proposes an enhanced framework using Geographically and Temporally Weighted Regression (GTWR) to model layer-wise compaction. We integrate high-resolution SBAS-InSAR surface deformation data with sparse measurements from Multilayer Compaction Monitoring Wells (MLCWs). The GTWR model, calibrated using a spatiotemporal kernel, can successfully estimate layer-wise compaction regionally, providing valuable insights for sustainable groundwater management.\n\\---\n\n---\n\nEm th·∫•y sao?       "text": "v·∫≠y ƒë·∫ßu ti√™n em s·∫Ω copy ph·∫ßn Methodology ƒë·ªÉ √¥ng anh n·∫Øm b·∫Øt ƒë∆∞·ª£c em ƒëang l√†m g√¨ m·ªôt c√°ch real-time:\n\n---\n% \\section{Methodology}\n\n%\\subsection{SBAB-InSAR Processing}\n%\\label{subsec:sbas}\n\n\\subsection{Geographically Temporally Weighted Regression}\n\\label{subsec:gtwr}\n\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n\n\\subsubsection{Model Calibration}\n\\label{subsec:gtwr_calib}\n%\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n% Section: Recap GWR concept\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n%\nBefore discussing the GTWR model, it is useful to review the fundamental concepts of ordinary least squares (OLS) regression and geographically weighted regression (GWR). The OLS models the relationship between a dependent variable and one or more predictors, under the assumption that the relationships being modeled are spatially stationary, expressed as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\ty_i \u003d \\sum_{k\u003d0}^{p} \\beta_k x_{ik} + \\varepsilon_i\n\t\\label{eq:ols}\n\\end{equation}\n\n\\noindent where \\(y_i\\) denotes the dependent variable at the \\(i\\)-th location; \\(x_{ik}\\) represents the independent variables (or predictors), with \\(x_{i0} \u003d 1\\) for the intercept \\(\\beta_0\\); \\(\\beta_k\\) indicates the regression coefficient corresponding to the \\(k\\)-th predictor; \\(\\varepsilon_i\\) is the random error at the \\(i\\)-th location, and \\(p\\) is the number of independent variables (excluding the intercept).\n%\nThe OLS method assigns a single set of coefficients to all observations regardless of their geographical locations, which deliberately neglects potential spatial variation and can cause model misspecification \\citet{mgwr_book_2017}.\n%\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n% Section: Recap GWR concept\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n%\nThe GWR approach allows the regression coefficients of the predictors to vary at different locations, thereby capturing local variations \\citep{brunsdon1996, fotheringham2002geographically}. The GWR is formulated as follows:\n\n\\begin{equation}\ny_i \u003d \\sum_{k\u003d0}^{p} \\beta_k(u_i, v_i) x_{ik} + \\varepsilon_i\n\\label{eq:gwr}\n\\end{equation}\n\n\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n% Section: Introduce GTWR\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n\n%\\(x_{i0} \u003d 1\\) for the intercept, \\((u_i, v_i)\\) are the spatial coordinates of the \\(i\\)-th point, \\(\\beta_k(u_i, v_i)\\) and \\(x_{ik}\\) are the coefficient and observed value for the \\(k\\)-th independent variable, respectively; \\(\\varepsilon_i\\) is the random error, and \\(p\\) is the number of independent variables (excluding the intercept). \n\n\\noindent where the coefficients appear similar to those in an OLS model. However, the key difference is that the GWR coefficients \\(\\beta_k\\) are indexed by the spatial coordinates \\((u_i, v_i)\\) of the \\(i\\)-th point. \\Cref{eq:gwr} indicates that the relationships between dependent and independent variables are spatially nonstationary, which provides a more realistic model than the global regression, which assumes spatially constant relationships. However, the environmental quantities not only exhibit spatial but also temporal relationships. Therefore, \\citet{huang2010geographically} introduced the GTWR model to account for spatiotemporal nonstationarity. The general form of the GTWR model can be expressed as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\ny_i \u003d \\sum_{k\u003d0}^{p} \\beta_k(u_i, v_i, t_i) x_{ik} + \\varepsilon_i\n\\label{eq:gtwr}\n\\end{equation}\n\nThe GTWR terms mirror those in GWR, adding the temporal dimension $t_i$. The GTWR model is calibrated based on the assumption that observations closer to the $i$-th point in spatiotemporal coordinate system have greater influence on the estimation of $\\beta_k(u_i, v_i, t_i)$ than more distant ones. The estimated parameters $\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\beta}}(u_i, v_i, t_i)$ can be obtained by:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\beta}}(u_i, v_i, t_i) \u003d (\\mathbf{X}^T\\mathbf{W}(u_i, v_i, t_i)\\mathbf{X})^{-1}\\mathbf{X}^T\\mathbf{W}(u_i, v_i, t_i) \\mathbf{y}\n\\label{eq:coeff_gtwr}\n\\end{equation}\n\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n% emphasize the weight matrix construction\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n\\noindent where $\\mathbf{W}(u_i, v_i, t_i)$ is a diagonal matrix with elements denoting the spatiotemporal weights assigned to the observations associated with the $i$-th point. The weights are usually obtained through a kernel function that implement either a fixed bandwidth (using specific distance thresholds) or an adaptive bandwidth (seeking an optimal number of nearest observations), as described by \\citet{Paez2002Framework}. A fixed bandwidth can introduce estimation bias dependent on observation density. More specifically, in regions with dense observations, an excessive number of neighbors might lead to over-smoothing, while an insufficient number is more likely to cause uncertain estimates. The adaptive bandwidth ensures each regression point is informed by exactly the same number of neighbors, regardless of data density. Due to the sparse distribution of the MLCW stations across the study area, this study employed the adaptive bi-square kernel function, defined as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\nW_{ij} \u003d \\begin{cases} \n\t[1 - (d^{ST}_{ij}/h_i)^2]^2, \u0026 \\text{if } d^{ST}_{ij} \u003c h_i \\\\\n\t0, \u0026 \\text{otherwise}\n\\end{cases}\n\\label{eq:bisquare}\n\\end{equation}\n\n\\noindent where $d^{ST}_{ij}$ denotes the spatiotemporal distances between location $i$ and $j$, and $h_i$ is the adaptive bandwidth. Since spatial and temporal distances are measured in different units and exhibit different scale effects, \\citet{Wu2014Geographically} proposed an integrated formulation, expressed as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\\begin{cases}\n\td_{ij}^{\\text{ST}} \u003d \\lambda d_{ij}^{\\text{S}} + (1-\\lambda) d_{ij}^{\\text{T}} + 2 \\sqrt{\\lambda (1-\\lambda) d_{ij}^{\\text{S}} d_{ij}^{\\text{T}}} \\cos(\\xi), \u0026 t_j \u003c t_i \\\\\n\td_{ij}^{\\text{ST}} \u003d \\infty, \u0026 t_j \u003e t_i\n\\end{cases}\n\\label{eq:st_dist}\n\\end{equation}\n\n\\noindent where $t_i$ and $t_j$ are sampled times at locations $i$ and $j$; \\( \\lambda \\text{ and } \\xi \\in [0, \\pi] \\) are adjustment parameters. Noticeably, \\Cref{eq:st_dist} implies that the GTWR model will simplify to GWR (\\cref{eq:gwr}) when $\\lambda\u003d1$, and space-time interaction effects reach their maximum when $\\xi\u003d0$. In practice, the bandwidths $h_i$, $\\lambda$, and $\\xi$ can be determined and optimized using a corrected version of the Akaike Information Criterion (AICc) \\citep{Hurvich2002_AICc}, defined as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\t\\text{AICc} \u003d 2n \\log_e (\\hat{\\sigma}) + n \\log_e (2\\pi) + n \\left\\{ \\frac{n + \\text{tr}(\\mathbf{S})}{n - 2 - \\text{tr}(\\mathbf{S})} \\right\\}\n\\label{eq:aicc}\n\\end{equation}\n\n\\noindent where \\(n\\) is the sample size, \\(\\hat{\\sigma}\\) is the estimated standard deviation of the error term, and \\(\\text{tr}(\\mathbf{S})\\) denotes the trace of the hat matrix $\\mathbf{S}$. The hat matrix $\\mathbf{S}$ is defined as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\t\\mathbf{S} \u003d \\{S_{ij}\\}, \\text{ where } S_{ij} \u003d \\mathbf{x}_i^T(\\mathbf{X}^T \\mathbf{W}(u_i, v_i, t_i) \\mathbf{X})^{-1}\\mathbf{X}^T \\mathbf{W}(u_i, v_i, t_i)\n\t\\label{eq:hat_matrix}\n\\end{equation}\n\n\\noindent  where $\\mathbf{x}_i^T$ represents the values of independent variables at the $i$-th point. The hat matrix $\\mathbf{S}$ transforms observed values into fitted values through spatiotemporal weightings, as in $\\hat{\\mathbf{y}} \u003d \\mathbf{S}\\mathbf{y}$, with its trace representing the effective number of parameters in the model \\citet{hat_matrix}. The detailed information of these components can be found in \\citet{fotheringham2002geographically}.\n\n% In this study, the parameters $\\lambda$ and $\\xi$ were set to $0.05$ and $0$, respectively, assuming that the temporal effect is dominant and space-time interactive effects are maximized. This configuration constrains the optimization process to focus on determining the optimal bandwidths $h_i$, thereby enhancing the computational efficiency during the calibration procedure.\n\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n\n\\subsubsection{Posterior Uncertainty Assessment}\n\\label{subsec:gtwr_prediction}\n\nWhen the optimal bandwidth is obtained through the calibration process, the fitted value $\\hat{\\mathbf{y}}$ at any unsampled location can be calculated using the hat matrix relationship as previously mentioned. The prediction error variance for each fitted value $\\hat{\\mathbf{y}}$ is defined as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\t\\sigma_i^2 \u003d \\hat{\\sigma}^2 \\left(1 + \\mathbf{x}_i^T (\\mathbf{X}^T \\mathbf{W}(u_i, v_i, t_i) \\mathbf{X})^{-1} \\mathbf{x}_i \\right)\n\t\\label{eq:gtwr_variance}\n\\end{equation}\n\n\\noindent where $\\hat{\\sigma}^2$ represents the overall model error variance, which is defined as:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\t\\hat{\\sigma}^2 \u003d \\frac{\\sum_{i\u003d1}^{N} (y_i - \\hat{y}_i)^2}{n - 2\\text{tr}(\\mathbf{S}) + \\text{tr}(\\mathbf{S}^T \\mathbf{S})}\n\t\\label{eq:model_error_variance}\n\\end{equation}\n\nThe first term in \\Cref{eq:gtwr_variance} accounts for the intrinsic model residual variance, while the second term assesses the additional uncertainty that arises when estimating local relationships from limited neighboring observations.\n\n\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n\n\\subsection{Curve Fitting}\n\\label{subsec:curve_fit}\n---",

      "text": "c√≤n ƒë√¢y l√† ph·∫ßn Data Set, ph·∫ßn n√≥i v·ªÅ MLCW v·∫´n ch∆∞a ho√†n thi·ªán, em s·∫Ω b·ªï sung th√™m, nh∆∞ng em mu·ªën √¥ng anh n·∫Øm ƒë∆∞·ª£c b·ª©c tranh hi·ªán t·∫°i. c√≤n ph·∫ßn Introduction, Results, Discussion, v√† conclusions v·∫´n ch∆∞a c√≥ ch·ªØ n√†o, em v·∫´n ƒëang suy nghƒ© ph·∫£i tr√¨nh b√†y c√°i g√¨ ƒë·ªÉ \"thu h√∫t\" ng∆∞·ªùi ƒë·ªçc. em r·∫•t lo l·∫Øng v√¨ b√†i c·ªßa em l√† ph√¢n t√≠ch d·ªØ li·ªáu ƒë∆°n thu·∫ßn, h∆°n l√† ƒë·ªÅ xu·∫•t m·ªôt l√Ω thuy·∫øt n√†o ƒë√≥.\n---\n% \u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\u003d\n\n\\subsection{Multilayer Compaction}\n\\label{subsubsec:mlcw}\n\nA multilayer compaction monitoring well (MLCW) is a specialized borehole extensometer that captures the subtle subsurface compaction by reading measurements at magnetic rings, strategically installed at boundaries between significant aquifers, or transitions between fine and coarse sedimentary materials, as defined by the Geological Survey and Mining Management Agency (GSMMA).  The depth of each MLCW often extends up to 300 m, with 21 to 26 magnetic rings anchored throughout the profile. Based on hydrogeological properties, aquifer units at each well are determined, each containing a number of corresponding magnetic rings, providing measurements of aquifer-specific compaction. The installation and measurement approaches of the MLCWs have been comprehensively described by \\citep{Hung2021_MLCW}. In this study, 29 MLCWs were employed, with monthly data collected from April 2016 to November 2021.\n\n\n\\subsection{Cumulative vertical displacement}\n\\label{subsubsec:vert_disp}\n\n% raw writing first, do not care about structure\n\nThis study derived cumulative vertical displacement from an 8-year dataset of Sentinel-1 SAR images \\text{(2016 - 2024)}, acquired from both ascending and descending orbits; the image properties are summarized in \\Cref{tab:sentinel1_info}. First, the images from each acquisition orbit were processed separately applying the \\textbf{\\textit{hyp3-isce2}} plugin, part of the Hybrid Pluggable Processing Pipeline (HyP3) \\citep{hyp3-isce2}. For interferogram formation, each SAR image was paired with up to four subsequent consecutive images to minimize temporal decorrelation. Next, each image pair was analyzed using the InSAR Scientific Computing Environment 2 (ISCE2) TOPS workflow, which sequentially performs burst-level coregistration of Single Look Complex (SLC) images, interferogram generation, waterbody masking and topographic phase correction with the Copernicus GLO-30 DEM, phase unwrapping via the SNAPHU algorithm, and geocoding to produce unwrapped interferograms suitable for further time-series InSAR analysis. A detailed description of the ISCE2 workflow is available in \\citep{isce2_rosen, tops, nesd_tops}.\n\n\\begin{table}[H]\n\t\\centering\n\t\\caption{Summary of the Sentinel-1A datasets used in this study.}\n\t\\label{tab:sentinel1_info}\n\t\n\t\\begin{tabular}{lcc}\n\t\t\\toprule\n\t\t\\textbf{Parameters} \u0026 \\textbf{Ascending} \u0026 \\textbf{Descending} \\\\\n\t\t\\midrule\n\t\tRelative Orbit (Path) \u0026 69 \u0026 105 \\\\\n\t\t\\multicolumn{1}{l}{Acquisition Period} \u0026 \\multicolumn{2}{c}{4/2016 ‚Äì 11/2021} \\\\\n\t\tNumber of Images      \u0026 266 \u0026 264 \\\\\n\t\t\\multicolumn{1}{l}{Acquisition Mode} \u0026 \\multicolumn{2}{c}{Interferometric Wide (IW)} \\\\\n\t\t\\multicolumn{1}{l}{Polarization} \u0026 \\multicolumn{2}{c}{VV} \\\\\n\t\tIncidence Angles \u0026 32¬∞ ‚Äì 38¬∞ \u0026 38¬∞ ‚Äì 43¬∞ \\\\\n\t\tSatellite Headings \u0026 347.63¬∞ \u0026 192.37¬∞ \\\\\n\t\t\\bottomrule\n\t\\end{tabular}\n\\end{table}\n\nSubsequently, a time-series analysis was conducted on the unwrapped interferograms from ascending and descending orbits separately using the small baseline subset approach, provided by Miami InSAR Time-series software (MintPy) \\citep{mintpy_yunjun}. The valid interferogram network was formed through a two-stage selection. The minimum spanning tree (MST) \\citep{mst_perissin} first identified the most coherent interferograms to connect all SAR images, by using the inverse of the average spatial coherence of all interferograms as weight. After this, any interferograms not included in the MST were excluded if their average spatial coherence was lower than $\\text{0.3}$. After network formation, the optimal values of the interferometric phase timeseries were estimated through a network inversion using the inverse of the phase variance as weight \\citep{networkinverse_var}. The temporal coherence, as a product of the network inversion process, was utilized to evaluate the reliability of the estimated value at each pixel \\citep{temporal_coherence}. Pixels with temporal coherence ($\\gamma_\\textit{temp}$) below 0.65 were then masked from further processing. Regarding atmospheric correction, the tropospheric delay components were removed based on the empirical linear relationship between InSAR phase delay and elevation \\citep{height_correlation}. All interferograms from both ascending and descending orbits were referenced to a single stable point, which was located far from the subsiding area (\\Cref{fig:insar_leveling_a}). Finally, the line-of-sight deformation (${d_\\textit{LOS}}$) was decomposed into the east-west (${d_\\textit{E-W}}$) and vertical (${d_\\textit{U}}$) components, assuming the deformation in north-south direction (${d_\\textit{N-S}}$) was negligible. The decomposition employed the incidence angles ($\\theta$) and satellite heading angles ($\\alpha$) from both ascending ($\\textit{asc}$) and descending ($\\textit{desc}$) images \\citep{hanssen_book}:\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\t\\begin{bmatrix}\n\t\td_{\\textit{U}} \\\\\n\t\td_{\\textit{E-W}}\n\t\\end{bmatrix}\n\t\u003d\n\t\\begin{bmatrix}\n\t\t\\cos(\\theta^{\\text{asc}}) \u0026 \\sin(\\theta^{\\text{asc}}) \\cos(\\alpha^{\\text{asc}}) \\\\\n\t\t\\cos(\\theta^{\\text{desc}}) \u0026 \\sin(\\theta^{\\text{desc}}) \\cos(\\alpha^{\\text{desc}})\n\t\\end{bmatrix}^{-1}\n\t\\begin{bmatrix}\n\t\td_{\\textit{LOS}}^{\\text{asc}} \\\\\n\t\td_{\\textit{LOS}}^{\\text{desc}}\n\t\\end{bmatrix}.\n\t\\label{eq:decomposition} %\u003c-- Correct placement and spelling\n\\end{equation}\n\n\nValidation of the vertical displacements was performed against precise leveling survey, with benchmarks locations shown in \\Cref{fig:studyarea}. First, reliable measurement points ($\\gamma_\\textit{temp} \\ge 0.65$) within a 200 m radius of each benchmark were selected. Average velocities were then derived for these points from both the InSAR and annual leveling survey time series, calculated as the slope of the best-fitting line to their respective displacement time series. Such a comparison based on rates was required due to the different temporal sampling intervals of the two datasets \\Cref{fig:insar_leveling}.\n\n\\begin{figure}[H]\n\t\\centering\n\t\n\t% --- Left Subplot (Figure A) ---\n\t\\begin{subfigure}[b]{0.3\\textwidth}\n\t\t\\centering\n\t\t\\framebox{\\rule{0pt}{8cm}\\rule{\\textwidth}{0pt}}\n\t\t\\caption{} % \u003c-- ADD THIS BACK.       "text": "em kh√¥ng hi·ªÉu ƒëo·∫°n n√†y trong b√†i b√°o c·ªßa Reyhan, n√≥ l√† g√¨ v·∫≠y?\n\n---\n\\documentclass[a4paper,12pt]{article}\n\\usepackage[utf8]{inputenc}\n\\usepackage[T1]{fontenc}\n\\usepackage{amsmath}\n\\usepackage{geometry}\n\\geometry{margin\u003d1in}\n\n% Defining mathematical notation and equations\n% Establishing the framework for horizontal sensitivity analysis\n\n\\begin{document}\n\n\\section{Horizontal Sensitivity Among MLCW-pairs}\n\nGiven the natural tendency of groundwater systems to extend horizontally, influencing water flow and pressure fluctuations (Winter et al., 1998; Wang et al., 2014; Gorelick and Ge, 2015), it is imperative to investigate the horizontal sensitivity across different wells. For this purpose, we established a horizontal sensitivity model utilizing six MLCW profiles (Fig. 1), extending from the groundwater recharge area in the east to the coastal region in the west, with two profiles in the Changhua area and four in the Yunlin area, chosen to study regions with significant subsidence and vertical sensitivity nuances. Building on a concept similar to vertical sensitivity analysis, we employ normalized cross-correlation analysis to assess the reactivity of distinct layers to changes in groundwater levels among MLCW pairs. In our approach, we designated MLCWs located upstream as reference or groundwater sources to be paired with downstream MLCWs as targets or directions of groundwater flow. Calculations were conducted to observe each source layer\u0027s interaction with the target MLCW\u0027s overall layers (Fig. 11).\n\nTo elucidate the interrelationships of horizontal sensitivities across the MLCW-pairs, we have developed and implemented a horizontal sensitivity model along each MLCW profile (Fig. 12) using the interpolation method of Wang and Wright (2012) and Walters et al. (2014).\n\n\\subsection{Methodology Details}\n\nIntegrating the horizontal normalized cross-correlation matrices from selected MLCW pairs to form an elongated profile. We first established a mesh of arbitrary spherical triangles, 400 m in length and 5 m in depth, along the entire profile, assuming that the horizontal cross-correlation coefficient is constant within each triangle. We determined the optimized cross-correlation for each grid point across the profile by interpolating the whole selected cross-correlations using the least squares (Eq. (3)).\n\n\\begin{equation}\n\\left[ \\kappa^2 \\nabla^2 \\right] [m_{cc}] \u003d \\left[ \\begin{array}{c} Wd_{MLCW} \\\\ 0 \\end{array} \\right]\n\\end{equation}\n\nwhere \\( Wd \\) is the weighted horizontal cross-correlation coefficient matrix of whole MLCW pairs along the profile, \\( WG \\) is the weighted Green\u0027s function for design matrix consisting of the shape function for MLCW cross-correlation, \\( \\kappa^2 \\) is the smoothing factor that determines the Laplacian smoothing, and \\( \\nabla^2 \\) is scale-dependent umbrella operation, a discretized approximation of the Laplacian smoothing operation (Desbrun et al., 1999). Crucially, we imposed two critical constraints on our model: layer pairs were selected with depth differences of less than 40 m as weighting (\\( W \\)) (Eq. (4)).\n\n\\begin{equation}\nW \u003d \\frac{1}{\\exp\\left(\\frac{(x_2 - x_1)^2}{\\sigma_x^2}\\right)}\n\\end{equation}\n\nwhere \\( x_1 \\) and \\( x_2 \\) represent the layer depths of the MLCW target and source, respectively, while \\( \\sigma_x \\) denotes the distance-decay constant, taken as half of the layer pairs with depth differences. The choice of a 40-m depth difference as a threshold is substantiated by the consistent hydrogeological layer transitions observed at the depth range across the hydrogeological profiles (Central Geological Survey (CGS), 1999), indicating a reliable regional stratigraphic marker. The threshold for horizontal cross-correlation was set at the 3rd percentile (\u003e75\\%), which is set to ensure that the most significant subsurface interactions are captured and to enhance the model\u0027s physical realism. This stringent cutoff selects for robust, meaningful connections critical for accurate hydrogeological modeling. These constraints were vital in ensuring the model\u0027s realistic representation of physical relationships and capturing strong and meaningful subsurface interaction.\n\nA critical focus in developing our model was selecting and applying the smoothing factor, \\( \\kappa^2 \\). In the trade-off test, we evaluated \\( \\kappa^2 \\) values from 1 to 0.5. At \\( \\kappa^2 \u003d 1 \\), the model showed higher smoothing but reduced sensitivity to local variations, while \\( \\kappa^2 \u003d 0.5 \\), the model increased sensitivity with a risk of overfitting. Our optimal range is between 0.7 and 0.8, balancing local accuracy and generalization. This range was corroborated by comparisons with the vertical sensitivity model, indicating the best alignment in the model. To assess the veracity and dependability of the models, we implemented the bootstrap resampling technique, systematically generating 10,000 bootstrap replicates from our original dataset. This methodology facilitates a precise estimation of the underlying statistical distributions and enhances the robustness of the model. It concurrently quantifies the uncertainties associated with each grid point, thereby augmenting the model\u0027s analytical rigor.\n\nIn conjunction with analyzing the variability in MLCW data, we also examined horizontal groundwater level oscillations along the profile (Fig. 12). This is predicated on the hypothesis that layer compaction disturbances are a consequence of groundwater level fluctuations. We implemented the normalized cross-correlation technique on the collected groundwater level data to discern the horizontal sensitivity of the well pairs after eliminating the long-term trends in groundwater levels. Operating under the premise that the electric resistivity variations are indicative of alterations in groundwater structure (Taylor et al., 1991; Auken et al., 2003; Sharlov et al., 2019), we further incorporated observations derived from the Transient Electromagnetic Method (TEM) conducted in the Yunlin area (Kassie et al., 2023) (Fig. 12). TEM is effective for groundwater exploration due to its sensitivity to low-resistivity layers like aquifers ensuring reliable results across diverse environments (Kassie et al., 2023). These TEM observations were used to validate our horizontal sensitivity models. In hydrogeology, low resistivity (6-40 \\(\\Omega\\)-m) indicates impermeable aquitards, while high resistivity (\u003e50 \\(\\Omega\\)-m) suggests permeable aquifers (Kassie et al., 2023).\n\n\\end{document}\n---",

(Your Analysis Method)**\n\nYou have chosen **Geographically and Temporally Weighted Regression (GTWR)**.\n\n*   **How does it work in principle?** GTWR is an advanced local regression technique. Instead of finding a single, \"one-size-fits-all\" relationship between InSAR (your predictor) and MLCW (your target) for the entire region, GTWR builds **thousands of tiny, individual regression models**. For each specific point in space (x, y) and time (t), it finds a unique relationship (`Œ≤` coefficient) based on a small subset of the \"nearest\" data points in a 4D (space + time) context.\n*   **What\u0027s special about it, and why did you choose it?** You chose this method for two critical reasons that directly address the weaknesses of simpler models:\n    1.  **It Handles Spatial Heterogeneity:** It doesn\u0027t assume the relationship between surface and subsurface is the same everywhere. It allows this relationship to change from Changhua to Yunlin, perfectly suited for tackling the study area\u0027s paradox.\n    2.  **It Handles Temporal Non-stationarity:** It doesn\u0027t assume the relationship is constant over time. It allows the physical \"sensitivity\" (`Œ≤` coefficient) of the aquifer system to change between wet and dry seasons, or during a severe drought. This is crucial for capturing the dynamic, non-linear behavior of the subsurface, such as **hysteresis**.\n\nYou chose GTWR not just because it\u0027s a fancy tool, but because it is the **right tool** to investigate a problem that is inherently variable in both space and time.\n\n---\n\nThis is the end of Part One.
